  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2e601318-1fbf-4d29-a183-ca7fe89b1cfb'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13755939351841612134">From the author of the beloved #1 national bestseller <b>Crow Lake </b>comes an exceptional new novel of jealously, rivalry and the dangerous power of obsession.<br/><br/>Two brothers, Arthur and Jake Dunn, are the sons of a farmer in the mid-1930s, when life is tough and another world war is looming. Arthur is reticent, solid, dutiful and set to inherit the farm and his father’s character</span>
  <span id="freeText13755939351841612134" style="display:none">From the author of the beloved #1 national bestseller <b>Crow Lake </b>comes an exceptional new novel of jealously, rivalry and the dangerous power of obsession.<br /><br />Two brothers, Arthur and Jake Dunn, are the sons of a farmer in the mid-1930s, when life is tough and another world war is looming. Arthur is reticent, solid, dutiful and set to inherit the farm and his father’s character; Jake is younger, attractive, mercurial and dangerous to know – the family misfit. When a beautiful young woman comes into the community, the fragile balance of sibling rivalry tips over the edge.<br /><br />Then there is Ian, the family’s next generation, and far too sure he knows the difference between right and wrong. By now it is the fifties, and the world has changed – a little, but not enough. <br /><br />These two generations in the small town of Struan, Ontario, are tragically interlocked, linked by fate and community but separated by a war which devours its young men – its unimaginable horror reaching right into the heart of this remote corner of an empire. With her astonishing ability to turn the ratchet of tension slowly and delicately, Lawson builds their story to a shocking climax. Taut with apprehension, surprising us with moments of tenderness and humour, <b>The Other Side of the Bridge</b> is a compelling, humane and vividly evoked novel with an irresistible emotional undertow.<br /><br /><i>Arthur found himself staring down at the knife embedded in his foot. There was a surreal split second before the blood started to well up and then up it came, dark and thick as syrup. <br /><br />Arthur looked at Jake and saw that he was staring at the knife. His expression was one of surprise, and this was something that Arthur wondered about later too. Was Jake surprised because he had never considered the possibility that he might be a less than perfect shot? Did he have that much confidence in himself, that little self-doubt? <br /><br />Or was he merely surprised at how easy it was to give in to an impulse, and carry through the thought which lay in your mind? Simply to do whatever you wanted to do, and damn the consequences.</i><br />–from <b>The Other Side of the Bridge</b><br /><br /><br /><i>From the Hardcover edition.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13755939351841612134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/54369435-darren-fajcz"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1570129985i/54369435._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/54369435-darren-fajcz">Darren Fajcz</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I wonder what the attraction is with her. She is painted as an untouchably perfect goddess for what seems to be inexplicable reasons. Every male chara<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I wonder what the attraction is with her. She is painted as an untouchably perfect goddess for what seems to be inexplicable reasons. Every male character would throw themselves to the wolves for her and I guess I don't understand why. Honestly, she seems reprehensible for marrying Arthur, whom she clearly didn't love and then discovering (or admitting) years later that he was a good solid man and was worthy of her love all along. Her character is the only one in the book that rings hollow, which is a shame since she's central.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_930585016" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/930585016" />
    <a title="Angela M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m"><img alt="Angela M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512600770p2/4685500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/930585016?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela M" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m">Angela M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer930585016" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11061214893789673041">There is a place in northern Ontario, Canada called Struan. It’s a small town where the summers are green and young boys fish in Crow Lake and the winters are brutally cold and the roads are so deeply covered with snow that they keep people in. It’s a place where men are loggers, farmers or sawmill workers , or the one doctor caring for them all delivering babies, caring for sick children or trying to save the logger stabbed in a bar fight. It’s a place of families with good sons and bad sons, m</span>
  <span id="freeText11061214893789673041" style="display:none">There is a place in northern Ontario, Canada called Struan. It’s a small town where the summers are green and young boys fish in Crow Lake and the winters are brutally cold and the roads are so deeply covered with snow that they keep people in. It’s a place where men are loggers, farmers or sawmill workers , or the one doctor caring for them all delivering babies, caring for sick children or trying to save the logger stabbed in a bar fight. It’s a place of families with good sons and bad sons, mothers and fathers who seem to love one child more than the other, young men who lose their lives in the war fighting for their motherland against the Nazis. It’s a place where people stay, or where people leave and are drawn back by some inexplicable thing that makes them want to be there. This is the fictitious place that Mary Lawson has created for all three of her novels, but you could swear this must be a real place. Her writing draws you in so that you believe that you have travelled there.<br /><br />I first went to Struan when I read her first novel, Crow Lake (2003). I returned when I recently read, her latest novel Road Ends (2013). I don’t know how I missed The Other Side of the Bridge which was written in between these two in 2006, but I am so glad that I found it. This is the story of two brothers, Arthur and Jake, at odds throughout their lives and the tragedies that be fall them. Lawson has so skillfully developed these characters and we come to know them so well that the events seem inevitable. It is also about the woman who tears them even further apart. In many ways, it is also a coming of age story of a young man, named Ian, the son of the town Doctor whose mother has just abandoned him and he carries burdens of his own.<br /><br />The two stories mesh when Ian comes to work summers and weekends on Arthur’s farm. The narrative takes place over decades from the 1920’s to 1960’s, and the chapters alternate by date, but not in chronological order. It is through this mechanism, that Lawson tells us the story and even though the chapters are not in chronological order, it is not in the least bit confusing. Each chapter is captivating because it sheds light on the chapter before in some way.<br /><br />I was not expecting the tragic event that happens near the end, but yet there were some things that I thought were predictable. The story always drew me in – no matter how heartbreaking at times. As I mentioned earlier, I’m not sure how I missed this on publication, but I won’t let that happen with the next book by Mary Lawson, which I sincerely hope she is writing.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11061214893789673041" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4734485?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/198932-brian">Brian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4734485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7250932778018718506">This is one of the most emotionally satisfying books I have read. Set in a small town in the far north of Canada, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/129783.The_Other_Side_of_the_Bridge' title='The Other Side of the Bridge by Mary Lawson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Other Side Of The Bridge</a> is constructed of two parallel narratives: the story of Arthur, a farmer's son growing up in the nineteen thirties and forties; and Ian, a doctor's son growing up twenty years later. The two stories intersect when Ian, aged sixteen, comes to work on Arthur's farm. The Scotsman described Mary Lawson as a 'master of the quiet moment made significant, with a</span>
  <span id="freeText7250932778018718506" style="display:none">This is one of the most emotionally satisfying books I have read. Set in a small town in the far north of Canada, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/129783.The_Other_Side_of_the_Bridge" title="The Other Side of the Bridge by Mary Lawson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Other Side Of The Bridge</a> is constructed of two parallel narratives: the story of Arthur, a farmer's son growing up in the nineteen thirties and forties; and Ian, a doctor's son growing up twenty years later. The two stories intersect when Ian, aged sixteen, comes to work on Arthur's farm. The Scotsman described Mary Lawson as a 'master of the quiet moment made significant, with a tremedous eye for detail' and that both sums up her appeal and explains her success in creating such a believable community and such convincing characters that after reading this book I felt I wanted to go and see the place for myself and maybe look up the characters while I was there.</span>

<div id="review_2008190517" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2008190517" />
    <a title="Betsy Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5149179-betsy-robinson"><img alt="Betsy Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404493492p2/5149179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2008190517?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Betsy Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Betsy Robinson" href="/user/show/5149179-betsy-robinson">Betsy Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2008190517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7556717252511014662">This astoundingly simple but complicated story of sibling rivalry is the stuff of Greek tragedy, or Shakespeare, or Eugene O'Neill in the form of Mary Lawson's inimitable storytelling. Like her books <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1618339554' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Crow Lake</i>
</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1241837462' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Road Ends</i>
</a>, it takes place in the fictional northern Ontario town of Struan—a place so vivid that it becomes a running character in Lawson's books. Like the other books, the dialogue—and particularly the gaps filled with unspoken thoughts—is so real you can feel it breathe. In a</span>
  <span id="freeText7556717252511014662" style="display:none">This astoundingly simple but complicated story of sibling rivalry is the stuff of Greek tragedy, or Shakespeare, or Eugene O'Neill in the form of Mary Lawson's inimitable storytelling. Like her books <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1618339554" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Crow Lake</i>
</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1241837462" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Road Ends</i>
</a>, it takes place in the fictional northern Ontario town of Struan—a place so vivid that it becomes a running character in Lawson's books. Like the other books, the dialogue—and particularly the gaps filled with unspoken thoughts—is so real you can feel it breathe. In a novel, she manages to do what playwrights spend their lives honing: writing dialogue that conveys both action and all the unspoken thoughts and subtext. This takes bravery and inspiration for playwrights, but it takes heroic confidence for a novelist to trust that readers will still "get it." I get it. I love Mary Lawson and cannot wait for her to write another book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7556717252511014662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1476435548" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1476435548" />
    <a title="Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12287031-kiwi-begs2differ"><img alt="Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556698979p2/12287031.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1476435548?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎" href="/user/show/12287031-kiwi-begs2differ">Kiwi Begs2Differ  ✎</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19191491-the-other-side-of-the-bridge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12287031-kiwi-begs2differ?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1476435548" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12206554642453514719">What a find! The setting for this novel is a small community in rural northern Ontario, the timeframe spans a few decades, from the 30s with the great depression, through the Second World War to the early 60s. The main characters are the Dunn brothers: Arthur and Jake, as different as they could be, one plain, quiet and dependable, down to earth worker, the other handsome, risk taker and adventurous but also a trouble maker. Their story jumps around in time, but mainly follows two strands, one f</span>
  <span id="freeText12206554642453514719" style="display:none">What a find! The setting for this novel is a small community in rural northern Ontario, the timeframe spans a few decades, from the 30s with the great depression, through the Second World War to the early 60s. The main characters are the Dunn brothers: Arthur and Jake, as different as they could be, one plain, quiet and dependable, down to earth worker, the other handsome, risk taker and adventurous but also a trouble maker. Their story jumps around in time, but mainly follows two strands, one follows the two brothers growing up from childhood to teens. The second is set in a summer in the 60s, with Arthur, now adult, working their late father farm with the help of Ian, the son of the local doctor who is secretly attracted to Arthur’s wife beautiful wife Laura. These two time strands converge towards the end of the book, when Jake suddenly returns home.<br /><br />As the story develops, the underlying tension among the characters increases and culminates in a dramatic, albeit rather predictable, finale. It is a story of sibling rivalry, jealousy, duty, guilt, obsessive infatuation, personal choices and the weight of expectations. I loved the author’s believable characterisation and masterful storytelling. The audio narration by Paul Hecht was wonderful too.<br />This was my first novel by Mary Lawson, but it will not be my last. Highly recommended.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12206554642453514719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_67908244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67908244" />
    <a title="Jacki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1587840-jacki"><img alt="Jacki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1223297040p2/1587840.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/67908244?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacki" href="/user/show/1587840-jacki">Jacki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67908244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer986628808464342237">I read this book because I loved Crow Lake and just loved how it was written. Now I am a convert to Mary Lawson and I will read anything she puts out there. Hopefully she is somewhere right now working on a huge, fat book, because if I have any complaints it is that her books are so short! 300 pages of this one wasn't enough for me!<br/><br/>My favorite thing about this book was how it creeped up on the climax. The whole thing was written with such a calm, serene feel that when something does actually hap</span>
  <span id="freeText986628808464342237" style="display:none">I read this book because I loved Crow Lake and just loved how it was written. Now I am a convert to Mary Lawson and I will read anything she puts out there. Hopefully she is somewhere right now working on a huge, fat book, because if I have any complaints it is that her books are so short! 300 pages of this one wasn't enough for me!<br /><br />My favorite thing about this book was how it creeped up on the climax. The whole thing was written with such a calm, serene feel that when something does actually happen, it doesn't seem unbelievable. It seems like the whole book was flowing up to this point, like a river that (not surprisingly) ends up at the ocean. I liked how the whole book was subtile, but still really powerful.<br /><br />The characters were well developed and familiar. The setting was perfect. The whole thing just seemed well thought out and well put together. It all just seemed true. The feelings, the setbacks, the decisions... they just seemed real. <br /><br />I really enjoyed this one and look forward to other books by this author. </span>
  <a data-text-id="986628808464342237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2809138119" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2809138119" />
    <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt"><img alt="Rebecca McNutt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602390057p2/36977686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2809138119?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=canadian">canadian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=ontario">ontario</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2809138119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7900242550577518426">A lot of Canadian literature is rather dry, with fictional novels featuring mostly fishermen and coal miners in stories evoking nostalgia for very miserable careers, but <i>The Other Side of the Bridge</i> takes a whole different route into the tale of an Ontario town shattered by wartime grief and hidden drama. Sometimes dark but ultimately unforgettable, this novel is set in a fictional small town, one which could easily pass for real as it encompasses all the spirit and scenery of most other provinc</span>
  <span id="freeText7900242550577518426" style="display:none">A lot of Canadian literature is rather dry, with fictional novels featuring mostly fishermen and coal miners in stories evoking nostalgia for very miserable careers, but <i>The Other Side of the Bridge</i> takes a whole different route into the tale of an Ontario town shattered by wartime grief and hidden drama. Sometimes dark but ultimately unforgettable, this novel is set in a fictional small town, one which could easily pass for real as it encompasses all the spirit and scenery of most other provincial small towns here in Canada.<br /><br />Rather than try to pour in too much sentimentality or go the other direction into cynicism, Lawson's book keeps a good balance of both to keep things real. I was expecting something completely different than this, so it was great to find a book full of surprises, one that isn't afraid to run against the grain of genre expectations. While not my favourite literature from Canada, <i>The Other Side of the Bridge</i> is still an amazing book and totally worthwhile, and it has some scenic prose which is incredible and memorable at the same time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7900242550577518426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1767199520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1767199520" />
    <a title="Bonnie Brody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7630898-bonnie-brody"><img alt="Bonnie Brody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571268702p2/7630898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1767199520?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bonnie Brody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bonnie Brody" href="/user/show/7630898-bonnie-brody">Bonnie Brody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1767199520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9005625812854461695">This is the second book I've read by Mary Lawson but it won't be my last. Her writing style is one of depth and profundity. Her characterizations run true to life and I felt like I was in Struan, Canada among the book's characters.<br/><br/>Like 'Crow Lake', this book takes place on a farm in rural northern Canada. The protagonists are the Dunn family, especially Arthur and Jake, two brothers who could not be more different. Jake is a dilettante, a man who is without empathy or conscience, living his life</span>
  <span id="freeText9005625812854461695" style="display:none">This is the second book I've read by Mary Lawson but it won't be my last. Her writing style is one of depth and profundity. Her characterizations run true to life and I felt like I was in Struan, Canada among the book's characters.<br /><br />Like 'Crow Lake', this book takes place on a farm in rural northern Canada. The protagonists are the Dunn family, especially Arthur and Jake, two brothers who could not be more different. Jake is a dilettante, a man who is without empathy or conscience, living his life impulsively and for the moment. He is good-looking and his mother's favorite. Arthur, the older of the two, is serious and streadfast, a hard worker and a man of few words but deep emotions. It is Arthur that helps his father keep the farm going.<br /><br />When they are boys, Jake is involved in a serious accident that Arthur blames himself for. Though he saves Jake's life, Arthur wishes, at the time, that Jake would die. Arthur carries this guilt with him like an albatross onto adulthood. As the novel opens Arthur is married to the beautiful Laura and Jake is nowhere in the picture.<br /><br />Ian, the son of Struan's physician, asks Arthur for a summer job because he is smitten with Laura, Arthur's beautiful wife. Ian turns out to be a good worker and a special relationship develops between Ian and the Dunn family. <br /><br />This is a novel that explores the depths of human feelings, the sources of guilt and hope, while at the same time examining the every day workings of one family during the time prior to and after World War II. The novel switches back and forth between the 1930's and 1940's to the 1960's and the reader is slowly privy to the Dunn family history and experiences.<br /><br />There is not one slow page in this novel. It is heartrending, profound, and deep - nothing really lightweight about it except for Ian's fishing for the big one that got away. If you like well-developed characterization, drama, and angst, pick up this book and enjoy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9005625812854461695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_189664139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/189664139" />
    <a title="Adriane Devries" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5881079-adriane-devries"><img alt="Adriane Devries" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454960926p2/5881079.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/189664139?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adriane Devries" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adriane Devries" href="/user/show/5881079-adriane-devries">Adriane Devries</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385340380" href="/book/show/1746547.The_Other_Side_of_the_Bridge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5881079-adriane-devries?shelf=book-clubbing">book-clubbing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer189664139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1846190147182692156">Have you ever wept to come to the end of a book? I did today, when I finished our book club selection, The Other Side of the Bridge, by Mary Lawson. It was absolutely the best book I have ever read -- at least that’s how I feel today. There were many times throughout that I thought that if I could write the perfect book, this would be it: with the themes of duty and the benevolent traps of love and friendship, of the conflict between disappointment and contentment, and (how could I help but noti</span>
  <span id="freeText1846190147182692156" style="display:none">Have you ever wept to come to the end of a book? I did today, when I finished our book club selection, The Other Side of the Bridge, by Mary Lawson. It was absolutely the best book I have ever read -- at least that’s how I feel today. There were many times throughout that I thought that if I could write the perfect book, this would be it: with the themes of duty and the benevolent traps of love and friendship, of the conflict between disappointment and contentment, and (how could I help but notice) the trials of motherhood, all mixed up in the lives of characters set in a time and place gorgeously fraught with commingled beauty and despair. Lots of lovely foreshadowing throughout, as well. Another intriguing theme was Lawson’s treatment of the power and sanctity of Words. Whether words are written or expressed through action they become holy offerings in the trials of our momentous yet insignificant lives. <br /><br />Finally, after reading several books that have come up a little short, I’ve found an author that really takes me somewhere new, even for just a short time.<br /><br />Friends of mine have read another book of hers, Crow Lake, and said it was even better than this one, which I of course find hard to believe. If I could find more time to read as I used to before having kids, I would devour whatever else Lawson has to offer. As it is, I will keep faithfully reading my book club choices, hoping we find other treasures like this one in our quest.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1846190147182692156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_880673583" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/880673583" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/880673583?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Chrissie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/710529-lynne">Lynne</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2014-read">2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=ww2">ww2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=canada">canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer880673583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14417799849356163421"> In conclusion:<br/><br/>I liked this book for many reasons. It has humor. It covers widely varied topics, all of which I found interesting. Sibling rivalry. Parents’ attachments to their children…. and let’s admit it, we do not respond identically to each child. How do we / should we choose what we want to do with our lives? I mean what job we ultimately choose. Do we choose, or is it fate that decides for us? Are we destined for a certain occupation, given our particular personality? And what is the val</span>
  <span id="freeText14417799849356163421" style="display:none"> In conclusion:<br /><br />I liked this book for many reasons. It has humor. It covers widely varied topics, all of which I found interesting. Sibling rivalry. Parents’ attachments to their children…. and let’s admit it, we do not respond identically to each child. How do we / should we choose what we want to do with our lives? I mean what job we ultimately choose. Do we choose, or is it fate that decides for us? Are we destined for a certain occupation, given our particular personality? And what is the value of a job? Must we all be academics? The book is set in Canada during WW2, this too was interesting!<br /><br />The characters? Well there are several, but central to the story are two brothers and guess what - a girl they both like. One brother made me nervous just at the mention of his name. You have surely met such a person - handsome, charming ....but too good to be true. This is the one that made me so nervous! And I liked Pete, a Native American. There is Ian too, he works as a farm help. Each character ties the story together. One gripe I have with the book is that I do not understand why all the guys (at least three) are so captivated by Laura; I wish the author had drawn her better.<br /><br />The plot is definitely filled with excitement.<br /><br />Unfortunately this was another one of those books that do not run chronologically; it flips between different time periods. Each chapter begins with a date and newspaper headlines. The only thing achieved by this is an increase in suspense, or perhaps it is an attempt to turn the story into a mystery, into a puzzle to be solved? I personally could do without this.<br /><br />The narration by Paul Hecht was well done. He used different intonations for different characters.<br /><br />The book concludes with an author interview explaining how she went about writing the book and what she wanted to have said. Mary Lawson has plans for a third novel. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/129783.The_Other_Side_of_the_Bridge" title="The Other Side of the Bridge by Mary Lawson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Other Side of the Bridge</a> was her second following her first one <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8646.Crow_Lake" title="Crow Lake by Mary Lawson" rel="nofollow noopener">Crow Lake</a>.<br /><br />**************************<br /><br />First impressions: <br /><br />This is why I chose to read this book: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://theteatimereader.wordpress.com/2012/07/11/the-other-side-of-the-bridge-by-mary-lawson/">http://theteatimereader.wordpress.com...</a><br />Don't worry about spoilers.<br /><br />Immediately I love the writing. Each line is loaded with subtle humor. The theme, and why you might be interested? Who doesn't connect with the competing emotions between one sibling and another and parents' preferences for one child over another?<br /><br />I have just begun so let me see if this continues as well as it starts. <br /><br />BTW, this is set in the fictional town of Struan, Ontario, near New Liskeard, Ontario. Others have said this is set in northern Canada, well look at a map! It isn't. I certainly wouldn't place Ontario in northern Canada.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14417799849356163421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_5980525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5980525" />
    <a title="Pieter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/222379-pieter"><img alt="Pieter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187712969p2/222379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5980525?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pieter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pieter" href="/user/show/222379-pieter">Pieter</a>

  <span id="freeText7000709981873051944" style="display:none">I loved this sequel to Crow Lake. One is the hulking, quiet, empathetic farmer's son who has a competitive, trouble-making brother.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer767489261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16224976088591211449">The Other Side of the Bridge is a lovely little book. The story moves back and forth between the 1930s/40s when Arthur Dunn was growing up and the 1950s/60s when he was manning the family farm and the doctor's son Ian, was a part-time farmhand during high school.<br/><br/>There's rivalry, family connections, and love in this story. Mary Lawson's skill at portraying familial love in all it's interpretations is exquisite. She praises the unsung heroism of small town doctors in isolated communities. Lawson's</span>
  <span id="freeText16224976088591211449" style="display:none">The Other Side of the Bridge is a lovely little book. The story moves back and forth between the 1930s/40s when Arthur Dunn was growing up and the 1950s/60s when he was manning the family farm and the doctor's son Ian, was a part-time farmhand during high school.<br /><br />There's rivalry, family connections, and love in this story. Mary Lawson's skill at portraying familial love in all it's interpretations is exquisite. She praises the unsung heroism of small town doctors in isolated communities. Lawson's descriptive prose places us in the peaceful town of Struan where a simpler life doesn't guarantee peace of mind. Well worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16224976088591211449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_264492806" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/264492806" />
    <a title="Karen J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7471179-karen-j"><img alt="Karen J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537234023p2/7471179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/264492806?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen J." href="/user/show/7471179-karen-j">Karen J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780676977479" href="/book/show/1036814.The_Other_Side_of_the_Bridge">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Karen J. by: </span>
      SJHS Teacher Book Club
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer264492806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4129013352743290058">"This is a book you will be driven to share with friends."<br/><br/>So says The Gazette in Montreal, and I'm echoing it here.<br/><br/>This was November's book selection for the book club I never seem to get to, and because I hadn't finished the book by the time the meeting rolled around, and I didn't want it spoiled for me, I didn't go this time either. Having just finished it a few minutes ago, I am definitely glad I waited.<br/><br/>I read this book essentially in three sittings. I started it during a silent reading class</span>
  <span id="freeText4129013352743290058" style="display:none">"This is a book you will be driven to share with friends."<br /><br />So says The Gazette in Montreal, and I'm echoing it here.<br /><br />This was November's book selection for the book club I never seem to get to, and because I hadn't finished the book by the time the meeting rolled around, and I didn't want it spoiled for me, I didn't go this time either. Having just finished it a few minutes ago, I am definitely glad I waited.<br /><br />I read this book essentially in three sittings. I started it during a silent reading class at school, was unable to get back to it for a couple of weeks, read last night until I was afraid I'd fall asleep and lose my page, and then started reading again as soon as I woke up this morning. And I want to go back and read it again.<br /><br />I don't want to get into the plot particulars, so as not to spoil it for anyone else, but I will say a few things:<br /><br />* This author so realistically creates characters that they not only live and breathe, but you feel like you've known them your whole life and would not be surprised to find pictures of them in your photo album of snapshots from all of the little milestones life has to offer.<br /><br />* What impressed me the most, characterization aside, is the way the author puts in one tiny sentence that doesn't seem to mean much at the time. This sentence comes back to you with devastating clarity one hundred and fifty pages later, creating a sinking feeling in your gut as you know what you are about to read, don't really want to, and yet are compelled to do so.<br /><br />* There are almost no loose ends at the end of this book. So many interconnecting stories are told, and yet you are left with no real questions, save one. There is one character you don't know what ultimately happened to, and yet, the moment I thought of this, I realized it didn't matter. And that, in itself, was a statement.<br /><br />I am afraid to say anything else. Go read this book! You'll be very glad that you did!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4129013352743290058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_516484311" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/516484311" />
    <a title="Penny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3246569-penny"><img alt="Penny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509387548p2/3246569.jpg" /></a>

        <a title="Penny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Penny" href="/user/show/3246569-penny">Penny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3246569-penny?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3246569-penny?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer516484311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5420171381202249063">This is a superb book. It is set in northern Canada in a small town. The book is split into 2 stories that occur about 10 -15 years apart so that at the end they coincide. The narrative focuses in each story on teenage boys - Arthur and Ian. Each has difficulty facing the future, dealing with women and how to decide what kind of men they want to be.<br/><br/>In Ian's story he is working for the adult Arthur on his farm - Ian's father is a doctor, his mother hates where they live and Ian uses the farm as a</span>
  <span id="freeText5420171381202249063" style="display:none">This is a superb book. It is set in northern Canada in a small town. The book is split into 2 stories that occur about 10 -15 years apart so that at the end they coincide. The narrative focuses in each story on teenage boys - Arthur and Ian. Each has difficulty facing the future, dealing with women and how to decide what kind of men they want to be.<br /><br />In Ian's story he is working for the adult Arthur on his farm - Ian's father is a doctor, his mother hates where they live and Ian uses the farm as a place to escape the troubles at home. <br /><br />Meanwhile in Arthur's story we start with him as a young teenager. Although Arthur is the older brother, his younger brother Jake takes all the limelight and love in his home. Jake deliberately teases and slyly draws out those he targets, to persuade them that what he wants is really what they want too - this is especially so with girls. The one thing Jake despises is farming and farmers including his father. Arthur has to help out on the farm but Jake is always let off chores by his mother's intervention.<br /><br />The book in each narrative slowly burns to a crisis which impacts on all the characters. The women are empowered and then led astray, the boys try to make decisions that are decent and true but become less and less able to discern what is 'decent.'<br /><br />In the background is the landscape of rural Canada in all its harshness and beauty. Behind Arthur's story is world war II which devastates the young men of the area leaving Arthur abandoned and guilty. Behind both stories are the actions of Jake, both shown in the story and hinted at, which manipulate and manouvre the other characters.<br /><br />This is not a fast-paced action packed story. It is more like the landscape it descibes cold, fertile, 'beneath the surface' like the lake that Ian fishes on, yet beautiful and stark. The writing is deceptively simple and yet so amazing. Whilst it is an easy, accessible read it is not simplistic or facile, rather it is subtle and has depth.<br />I loved this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5420171381202249063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="fennie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="fennie" href="/user/show/229164-fennie">fennie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/229164-fennie?shelf=things-that-make-you-go-hmm">things-that-make-you-go-hmm</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112214462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9781240149944385577">i keep going back and forth as to whether 3 stars is appropriate for this book - it wasn't a page-turner with incessant drama that kept me up reading late into the night; the pace was slower, with a meandering path that not only told the tale of the characters, but gave life to the fictional town of struan. this story encompasses so much: unstated sibling rivalry, the effects of one's hometown, interpersonal relationships, family dynamics, guilt, expectations, rebelliousness, all mixed in with a</span>
  <span id="freeText9781240149944385577" style="display:none">i keep going back and forth as to whether 3 stars is appropriate for this book - it wasn't a page-turner with incessant drama that kept me up reading late into the night; the pace was slower, with a meandering path that not only told the tale of the characters, but gave life to the fictional town of struan. this story encompasses so much: unstated sibling rivalry, the effects of one's hometown, interpersonal relationships, family dynamics, guilt, expectations, rebelliousness, all mixed in with a bit of drama.<br /><br />some of the characters weren't as fully fleshed-out as i would have liked, but on the whole, that did not have adverse affects. my biggest issue was that i figured out one of the revelations too early, so when it was finally written out, i wasn't surprised, more annoyed that it occurred exactly as i had deduced.<br /><br />i found myself quite impressed by the author. she has a way of writing that makes you think about the human condition. things don't merely happen; there is a thought process behind everyone's actions, and therein lies the depth to this book. i will admit that there were certain instances where i was uncomfortable, but only because those events hit a little too close to home. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9781240149944385577" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6645241021361403944" style="display:none"><i>The Other Side of the Bridge<i> is a spell binding and edifying experience. I wept when I finished it because it rings so true. It is a story about sibling rivalry, friendship and loyalty. It is also a coming of age story that spans two generations. <br /><br />The writing is excellent. There is nothing there and doesn't belong or that distrubes the story telling in any way. There are no issues raised that aren't resolved. The characters are complete and multidemensional. What I like best about the writing is that it isn't at all preachy. We learn as much abouth the characters by seeing what they do as we do fromt he narative explanations of what is going on inside their heads. <br /><br /></i></i></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13491135603612717795">Just as with her book 'Crow Lake', I was instantly immersed into the two parallel story lines - one set in the past when Arthur and Jake are brothers growing up in Struan, Northern Ontario, two boys that couldn't be more different but nevertheless are tied by their family bond and outside expectations; the other set in the future where Arthur is a father and farmer, married to Laura, both seen through the eyes of their young farm help Ian, a boy who has to make his own difficult decisions and ha</span>
  <span id="freeText13491135603612717795" style="display:none">Just as with her book 'Crow Lake', I was instantly immersed into the two parallel story lines - one set in the past when Arthur and Jake are brothers growing up in Struan, Northern Ontario, two boys that couldn't be more different but nevertheless are tied by their family bond and outside expectations; the other set in the future where Arthur is a father and farmer, married to Laura, both seen through the eyes of their young farm help Ian, a boy who has to make his own difficult decisions and has to deal with his painful past as well. It's a slow moving narrative which is also compelling and carries an underlying tension that leads to a traumatic climax. <br />Highly recommended! </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/929773084?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/75157-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19555485-the-other-side-of-the-bridge">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75157-carol?shelf=five-star-rated">five-star-rated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75157-carol?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer929773084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12890342502654206458">This is a difficult task the writer has set for herself. She tells a large part of this story from the viewpoint of the least interesting character, Arthur. Such is her gift that even though we are seeing the world through the dumbfounded eyes of the good son who remains on the farm, we still understand the pain and complexity of the emotional life swirling around him, even though it is almost completely lost on Arthur. I'm not quite sure how Lawson carries it off but I can say you will not be d</span>
  <span id="freeText12890342502654206458" style="display:none">This is a difficult task the writer has set for herself. She tells a large part of this story from the viewpoint of the least interesting character, Arthur. Such is her gift that even though we are seeing the world through the dumbfounded eyes of the good son who remains on the farm, we still understand the pain and complexity of the emotional life swirling around him, even though it is almost completely lost on Arthur. I'm not quite sure how Lawson carries it off but I can say you will not be disappointed.<br /><br />Mary Lawson's marvelous “Crow Lake” was no fluke. “The Other Side of the Bridge” is even deeper and more devastating than her first novel. The pace of the story is slow but gripping. I read the entire story with my heart in my throat. There is MUCH sorrow here--war and farm life take a hard toll on innocence--and at the end you will feel as if you have lived an entire alternative life. Lawson's preoccupation with silent men doing their best is evident here, delivered with ever more complexity. Unforgettable.<br /><br />This is my book club's second pick of Ms. Lawson's and we all agree she is one of our favorite writers. We have wonderful discussions about her characters and feel we know them intimately. Soon, we will be reading “Road Ends” as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12890342502654206458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12742070748622556039" style="display:none">Couldn't put this one down and was hooked from the first chapter.<br /><br />From back cover:<br /><br />"The Other Side of the Bridge is an unforgettable story of jealousy, rivalry and the power of obsession played out over the course of a half century. Two brothers, the sons of a farmer living in the fictional Northern Ontario community of Struan and whose lives are overshadowed by the looming threat of World War II, are polar opposites. Arthur is shy, dependable and dutiful, set to inherit the family farm, while Jake, his younger brother, is handsome, moody and dangerous to know. When a beautiful young woman arrives in Struan, the fragile balance of the brothers' rivalry is pushed to the very edge of catastrophe. Taut with apprehension, surprising us with moments of tenderness and humour, this is a compelling and humane novel with a shocking climax."<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_92922917" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92922917" />
    <a title="Christina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/363558-christina"><img alt="Christina" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/92922917?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina" href="/user/show/363558-christina">Christina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer92922917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8460809672312000969">I didn't exactly choose to read this book - Mary Lawson's acclaimed follow-up to her first novel, Crow Lake. It would be more accurate to say I was sort of cornered into it. An English instructor at the small university where I work as a librarian asked me to give a research instruction workshop on literary databases for her students who had been assigned to write an analytical essay about the book. She (the instructor) had picked Lawson's book for the class because, as she put it, “it's a bit m</span>
  <span id="freeText8460809672312000969" style="display:none">I didn't exactly choose to read this book - Mary Lawson's acclaimed follow-up to her first novel, Crow Lake. It would be more accurate to say I was sort of cornered into it. An English instructor at the small university where I work as a librarian asked me to give a research instruction workshop on literary databases for her students who had been assigned to write an analytical essay about the book. She (the instructor) had picked Lawson's book for the class because, as she put it, “it's a bit more accessible than what we would normally read.” <br /><br />Let me start with a suggestion. Read a few chapters and if you aren't immediately hooked, put it down and give Annie Proulx a shot instead. There's nothing wrong with The Other Side of the Bridge, predictability and dullness aside. Take a Northern Ontario setting, a couple of brothers whose relationship is more than a little bit suggestive of that well used biblical dynamic (Jacob/Esau), rehash well-worn Can-lit themes: rural versus urban and the predictable character differences attributed to those who gravitate to one or the other (hint – the small town folks always, somehow, turn out to be the wiser if ostensibly less sophisticated ones), the setting itself (in Can-lit, the landscape is regularly featured as a stoic, austere, unforgiving yet inexplicably beautiful protagonist), and morals – the ones who have them versus the ones who don't, although it's never actually as simple as that, nor is it in this book. <br /><br />I know several people who would love The Other Side of the Bridge, and I know several more who would never ever read more than five pages of it. I'm holding back from writing, “if you liked W.O. Mitchell, you'll love this … “</span>
  <a data-text-id="8460809672312000969" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/353181357?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chaitra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chaitra" href="/user/show/9086236-chaitra">Chaitra</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9086236-chaitra?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer353181357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15096923369483241901">I'm conflicted on this book. I like Lawson, the portrayal of Struan, and of its people. I also liked the story of Ian, the child of a country doctor, destined to be one himself, but fighting against it tooth and nail. I did not like the older story, that of the sibling rivalry between Arthur and Jake, because I didn't much get a feel for the two men. The silent one is silent and plodding but somehow in the right, and the smarmy one is charming and utterly without morals. It can't be that simple?</span>
  <span id="freeText15096923369483241901" style="display:none">I'm conflicted on this book. I like Lawson, the portrayal of Struan, and of its people. I also liked the story of Ian, the child of a country doctor, destined to be one himself, but fighting against it tooth and nail. I did not like the older story, that of the sibling rivalry between Arthur and Jake, because I didn't much get a feel for the two men. The silent one is silent and plodding but somehow in the right, and the smarmy one is charming and utterly without morals. It can't be that simple? I needed to see inside Jake's head, but that wasn't forthcoming.<br /><br />But the reason I'm giving this a two star rating, much lower than I would have is that I disliked its women. They are by turns clingy, blind with affection, distant, cheating; none of them seem to make right choices. They are, all of them, opaque. For example, one of the driving forces of the book is Ian's mother's desertion of her husband and only son. We're told she loathed the place, and that she tried getting Ian to follow her. We're told she wrote countless letters after that fact, and that she cried on the phone with him. We're not privy to the phone conversations, and since Ian doesn't read a single letter from her, we don't know what she wrote. Were those not included because then she'd be rendered sympathetic to the readers? I don't know, but I do know that it detracted quite a bit from the book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15096923369483241901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_142879378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/142879378" />
    <a title="Lynne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1922690-lynne"><img alt="Lynne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394089619p2/1922690.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/142879378?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynne" href="/user/show/1922690-lynne">Lynne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer142879378&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer142879378" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10585718308606259630">This book was good, well-paced and interesting. It's been a while since I read the author's first book, Crow Lake, but it seems like the themes of this book are pretty similar - the kind of biblical tensions of sibling dynamics and the drama and sometimes violence that are apparently part of figuring out love for some folks. <br/><br/>I just re-read East of Eden by John Steinbeck, where there are also sets of brothers with tense relations. That book's message for me is that destiny doesn't control us, we </span>
  <span id="freeText10585718308606259630" style="display:none">This book was good, well-paced and interesting. It's been a while since I read the author's first book, Crow Lake, but it seems like the themes of this book are pretty similar - the kind of biblical tensions of sibling dynamics and the drama and sometimes violence that are apparently part of figuring out love for some folks. <br /><br />I just re-read East of Eden by John Steinbeck, where there are also sets of brothers with tense relations. That book's message for me is that destiny doesn't control us, we choose how we act and need to take responsibility for our choices even when people around us make life difficult. It seemed like these characters maybe needed a little dose of that message because this book's conclusion felt inevitable to me - like fate was driving it forward to the unavoidable and unsurprising climax - and that made me feel a bit frustrated. Carter's death was just all too pat, the ultimate punishment for Jake and Laura. But maybe that's the beauty of a book, that a story's purpose isn't necessarily to reflect reality but just rather to tell a story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10585718308606259630" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;

      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Her plan begins to fall in place when she enters high school and discovers she has a natural talent for excelling in shorthand, typing and other business classes. Meanwhile she spares no effort in helping her family continue to survive despite their poverty, a less than fertile few acres, and a family history of instability.She strives to fit in at high school in spite of the harsh limits placed on her by her hot-tempered, authoritarian mother, Corie Mae. She often turns for support to her easy-going father Ray, who sometimes intervenes to overrule Corie Mae’s restrictions.As she goes about her life, doing her school work and helping out at home, she interacts with interesting, unforgettable, and sometimes dangerous characters, including a mentally challenged neighbor, an escaped convict, and a lecherous employer. She is forced to make decisions and take actions that would be difficult for a much older adult. Maggie meets each challenge with determination, imagination, and courage whether it’s cutting a pitchfork from a mare’s tail or helping to deliver her baby sister.The typical spoken language, folkways, and traditional beliefs and religious practices are skillfully woven into this portrait of Appalachian family life. The author’s sympathetic insights into mountain culture combined with memorably etched characters and events create a realistic reflection of Tennessee mountain life during the decade following WWII.Maggie’s life takes an unexpected turn when her cousin JD reveals a dark secret that could shatter the family. Maggie struggles to maintain her dreams of a better life amidst the many trials that will test the grit of this Appalachian Daughter.“I absolutely loved the story! Dido and Harry are part of the cast of eccentric, utterly loveable characters, all transplants from elsewhere, who form an unlikely group at the station. Their loves and longings, their rivalries and entanglements, the stories of their pasts and what brought each of them to the North, form the centre. One summer, on a canoe trip four of them make into the Arctic wilderness (following in the steps of the legendary Englishman John Hornby, who, along with his small party, starved to death in the barrens in 1927), they find the balance of love shifting, much as the balance of power in the North is being changed by the proposed Mackenzie Valley gas pipeline, which threatens to displace Native people from their land.Elizabeth Hay has been compared to Annie Proulx, Alice Hoffman, and Isabel Allende, yet she is uniquely herself. What unfolds is a completely enveloping story that spans a few pivotal years of his youth. Moving from city to country, summer to winter, wellbeing to illness, the novel charts the deepening bond between mother and son even as the family comes apart. \n\nSet in the mid-1990s, when Quebec is on the verge of leaving Canada, this captivating novel is an unconventional coming of age story as only Elizabeth Hay could tell it. It draws readers in with its warmth, wisdom, its vivid sense of place, its searching honesty, and nuanced portrait of the lives of one family and those closest to it. Hay explores the mystery of how members of a family can hurt each other so deeply, and remember those hurts in such detail, yet find openings that shock them with love and forgiveness. Northwest Corner is a riveting story about the complex, fierce, ultimately inspiring resilience of families in the face of life’s most difficult and unexpected challenges.Twelve years after a tragic accident and a cover-up that led to prison time, Dwight Arno, now fifty, is a man who has started over without exactly moving on. Living alone in California, haunted yet keeping his head down, Dwight manages a sporting goods store and dates a woman to whom he hasn’t revealed the truth about his past. Then an unexpected arrival throws his carefully neutralized life into turmoil and exposes all that he’s hidden.Sam, Dwight’s estranged college-age son, has shown up without warning, fleeing a devastating incident in his own life. In its way, Sam’s sense of guilt is as crushing as his father’s. As the two men are forced to confront their similar natures and their half-buried hopes for connection, they must also search for redemption and love. In turn, they dramatically transform the lives of the women around them: the ex-wives, mothers, and lovers they have turned to in their desperate attempts to somehow rewrite, outrun, or eradicate the past.Told in the resonant voices of everyday people gripped in the emotional riptide of lived life, Northwest Corner is at once tough and heart-lifting, an urgent, powerful story about family bonds       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5619.Mary_Lawson">About Mary Lawson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5619.Mary_Lawson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1253559594p3/5619.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5287045799833756473">Mary Lawson (born 1946) is a Canadian novelist.<br/><br/>Born in southwestern Ontario, she spent her childhood in Blackwell, Ontario (located between Sarnia and Brights Grove) and is a distant relative of L. M. Montgomery, author of Anne of Green Gables.<br/><br/>Lawson moved to England after graduating from McGill University with a psychology degree in 1968. She also married in Ontario, has two grown up sons and now</span>
  <span id="freeText5287045799833756473" style="display:none">Mary Lawson (born 1946) is a Canadian novelist.<br /><br />Born in southwestern Ontario, she spent her childhood in Blackwell, Ontario (located between Sarnia and Brights Grove) and is a distant relative of L. M. Montgomery, author of Anne of Green Gables.<br /><br />Lawson moved to England after graduating from McGill University with a psychology degree in 1968. She also married in Ontario, has two grown up sons and now lives in Kingston-Upon-Thames, Surrey. Her three novels to date, both published by Knopf Canada were set in Northern Ontario.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5287045799833756473" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/25378-the-other-side-of-the-bridge">Quotes from The Other Side of...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Suddenly he saw himself as others in the crowd must surely see him; a silent, solitary figure, standing apart from the rest. He looked out at the hoardes of singing, laughing people and felt more alone than he'd ever felt in his life. Was this how it was going to be then? Was this who he was? A man apart from his fellows, making the journey through life alone?&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/525058-suddenly-he-saw-himself-as-others-in-the-crowd-must">60 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You'd have thought that after suffering such a loss nothing else would matter to her but that didn't seem to be how it worked. She was fearful about everything now. It was as if she had finally seen the awful power of fate, it's deviousness, the way it could wipe out in an instant the one thing you had been certain you could rely on, and now she was constantly looking over her shoulder, trying to work out where the next blow might fall.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/525055-you-d-have-thought-that-after-suffering-such-a-loss-nothing">6 likes</a>
            </nobr>
